In this research, the current and future situation of final disposal of MSW in Mexico was studied, focusing on the spatial and temporal evaluation of final disposal sites (FDS), landfill gas (LFG) emissions, and potential power generation in an 80-year horizon. Geographic information systems were applied for spatial evaluation. The Mexico LFG 2.0 model was used to estimate the LFG emissions in 1782 FDS in operation, considering statal MSW characteristics and local FDS features. The transition towards a MSW management system that is less dependent on final disposal was carried out via a sensitivity analysis of the reduction of FDS in LFG emissions, power generation, and greenhouse gases (GHG) emissions. In this research, the current and future situation of final disposal of MSW in Mexico was studied, focusing on the spatial and temporal evaluation of final disposal sites (FDS), landfill gas (LFG) emissions, and potential power generation in an 80-year horizon. Geographic information systems were applied for spatial evaluation. The Mexico LFG 2.0 model was used to estimate the LFG emissions in 1782 FDS in operation, considering statal MSW characteristics and local FDS features. The transition towards a MSW management system that is less dependent on final disposal was carried out via a sensitivity analysis of the reduction of FDS in LFG emissions, power generation, and greenhouse gases (GHG) emissions. Register now\nCurrent State of Waste Management in Mexico City\nMexico City is the third most populated agglomeration in the world. Amongst megacities, Mexico City occupies second place on most waste generation, after the New York region. The city\u2019s waste generation can be linked to the high consumption and use of resources, consequence of the rapid pace of life.\nby Diana Fernanda Butron B.\nWaste Management in Mexico City is carried by three different groups: public cleaning services, private companies, and informal workers. Informal workers referring to the ones that are not formally registered as employed by the government or private company and work independently. The public cleaning services collect and execute the final disposal of the public solid waste. Private companies do the same, but for businesses and industries. Informal workers perform manual separation of the municipal solid waste. In addition, privately hired street sweepers and municipal employees negotiate and coordinate their tasks with the purpose of complementing each other and to avoid overlapping. However, official reports of waste management report only the public cleaning services as the supplier...",

This means that \non average, every citizen produces one kilo of solid waste every day. Since the start of \nthe COVID -19 pandemic, this number has started to rise. The city is expected to generate \n3 thousand tonnes more garbage than usual as citizens quarantine at home. This \nmeans that one of the biggest problems facing the country is that of the excessive generation of waste.\nMexico City, an enormous sprawling city, has struggled with waste for many years. It now \nranks as the second biggest producer of waste among the world\u2019s megacities (behind New York City), with waste management systems that cannot keep up with the problem. For most households in the capital, the biggest culprit is kitchen waste , including \nvegetable, fruit, tortilla and bread waste. In second place we find plastics, often in the \nform of single -use products, like plastic bottles and disposable plastic bags and \npackaging. The use of these products have increased during the pandemic as sanitation and convenience is prioritized. Lastly, even though e -waste only represents less than 1% \nof the total amount of waste produced in Mexico, this number is expanding rapidly. \nStudies show that 53.1 million tonnes of waste is produced in all of Mexico annually. With \n14.9 million tonnes that could be reused, the lack of infrastructure and regulations in the country have made it difficult to implement new initiatives. This means that \non average, every citizen produces one kilo of solid waste every day. Since the start of \nthe COVID -19 pandemic, this number has started to rise. The city is expected to generate \n3 thousand tonnes more garbage than usual as citizens quarantine at home. This \nmeans that one of the biggest problems facing the country is that of the excessive generation of waste.\nMexico City, an enormous sprawling city, has struggled with waste for many years. It now \nranks as the second biggest producer of waste among the world\u2019s megacities (behind New York City), with waste management systems that cannot keep up with the problem. For most households in the capital, the biggest culprit is kitchen waste , including \nvegetable, fruit, tortilla and bread waste. In second place we find plastics, often in the \nform of single -use products, like plastic bottles and disposable plastic bags and \npackaging. The use of these products have increased during the pandemic as sanitation and convenience is prioritized. Lastly, even though e -waste only represents less than 1% \nof the total amount of waste produced in Mexico, this number is expanding rapidly. \nStudies show that 53.1 million tonnes of waste is produced in all of Mexico annually. With \n14.9 million tonnes that could be reused, the lack of infrastructure and regulations in the country have made it difficult to implement new initiatives. Current programmes mostly focus on the country\u2019s city centres, which have better recycling infrastructure, \nwhile some outskirt areas still do not have a proper waste management system. \n4 WHAT DESIGN CAN DO NO WASTE CHALLENGE 2020\u2013 2021\n Image credits:\n5LOCAL INSIGHT\nINFORMAL LABOUR\nMEXICO CITY \n\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014\u2014Despite the fact that the government is in charge of local waste management systems, most of the cleaning workers in Mexico City are volunteer...",

Municipalities face the dual challenge of \nproviding infrastructure that improves quality of life but that does so at \nan affordable cost. Effective municipal waste management practices \nare limited in the developing world, causing public health problems and \npotent greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions. Waste to energy landfills are a \nsolution that harnesses this duality, providing numerous benefits such \nas proper disposal of waste and reduction of disease, plus capturing \nbiogas which can be put to productive use in the form of electricity \ngeneration, while also reducing the emission of harmful greenhouse \ngases. In turn, this electricity could potentially substitute traditional \nforms of energy used for public lighting or to fuel public works. This \nBrief examines how cities in Latin America have turned the problem of \nwaste management into an opportunity. It documents one of the first \nexperiences of low-carbon waste-management infrastructure - a waste \nto energy landfill - in the region, through a case study from Monterrey, \nMexico\u2019s second largest city. The story of Monterrey shows how the \ncity turned its landfill waste into electricity that moves its subway and \nprovides safer streets through public lighting, while at the same time \nshrinking municipalities\u2019 operating costs and tackling climate change \nby reducing GHG emissions.SUMMARYCase Study Brief\nWaste management is typically viewed as a \nresource intensive activity.
